[1mููุงูุด [0m

(1) Throughout the text, I use dogma advisedly, to highlight the problem of meaning in such descriptions as โmale dominanceโ or 
โfemale subordination.โ These are often used as articles of faith, assumed but not examined. For an attempt to explicate different 
types of โmale dominance,โ see Peggy Sandayโs [3mFemale Power and Male Dominance [0m(1981). As Foucault (1978: 93) points out, power โis 
not an institution, and not a structure: neither is it a certain strength we are endowed with; it is the name that one attributes 
to a complex strategical relationship in a particular society.โ 

(2) The differences between orientalism and occidentalism were brought to my attention through a fruitful discussion with my 
graduate student Saddeka Arebi of texts that were written in Arabic on the West. In this context it is instructive to refer to 
Eric Wolfโs [3mEurope and the People without History [0m. Wolf argues (1982: 6-7) that the categories of East and West encourage us to 
create false models of reality. However, the indigenous peoples of the East and the West use these two categories as folk 
categories that describe โthe other.โ 

(3) While this is generally true, some think that Boserupโs study advocates policy prescriptions no different from those of 
development agencies (see, for example, Beneria and Sen, 1981). 

(4) See also the discussion of this same point in Chapter 2, pp. 24โ50. 

(5) The male bias in ethnographic reporting is being documented. See for example, Nelson (1974), Pastner (1978), and also Rattray 
(1955: 84: quoted in Rogers, 1980: 145). When Rattrayโs informants commented that they did not stress the role of women to the 
anthropologists because โthe white man never asked us this; you have dealings with and recognize only men, we supposed the 
Europeans considered women of no account and we know you do not recognize them as we have always done!โ 

(6) See for example, Bullough (1973: especially pages 134-135). Stereotypes, by definition, may carry part truths or no truths. 
These areas of seeming contrast are based on the assumption that veiling, the cult of virginity, forced marriage, clitoridectomy 
and polygyny are characteristically โIslamic.โ These institutions predate Islam as Herodotus reported in the fifth century BC, and 
cross-cultural research reveals that they are known to different religions and cultures in Latin America, India, sub-Saharan 
Africa, Sri Lanka and Europe. 

(7) An exhibition in the College Library at Harvard University arranged by Alice C. Deyab in 1988 reported that women in the Arab 
world had published their own magazines for almost 100 years and these magazines then numbered over 300. In contrast to the owners 
of Western womenโs magazines, who were predominantly male, some 95% of those who owned, published, edited and wrote Arab womenโs 
magazines were female (Harvard University Library Notes, March 24, 1988). 

(8) The fact that women constitute the majority of the poor and impoverished is evident enough in American culture (see, e.g., 
Scott, 1984). In the Third World the same is true (see e.g., Sarri, 1985). Sarri discloses that, while women comprise more than 
half the worldโs population, they own less than 1% of its property and earn only 10% of its income. 

(9) See Diamond (1974) for a discussion of the notion of progress as inherent in Western civilization. 

(10) See also Hochschild (1989) for an analysis of the double shift problem for American women wage workers. 

(11) See also Ervin-Tripp [3met al. [0m, 1984. 

(12) See Johnson (1988) for a discussion of the dynamics of these two roles in American culture.
